      <p>
       <b>
        Modern history
       </b>
       , the
       <b>
        modern period
       </b>
       or the
       <b>
        modern era
       </b>
       , is the linear, global,
       <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
        historiographical
       </a>
       approach to the time frame after
       <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">
        post-classical history
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This view stands in contrast to the "organic," or non-linear, view of history first put forward by the renowned philosopher and historian,
       <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Spengler" title="Oswald Spengler">
        Oswald Spengler
       </a>
       , early in the 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Modern history can be further broken down into periods:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">
         early modern period
        </a>
        began approximately in the early 16th century; notable historical milestones included the European
        <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
         Renaissance
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">
         Age of Discovery
        </a>
        , and the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
         Protestant Reformation
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-4">
          [4]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlebyModern_5-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-bartlebyModern-5">
          [5]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Modern_history#Late_modern_period" title="Modern history">
         late modern period
        </a>
        began approximately in the mid-18th century; notable historical milestones included the
        <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
         French Revolution
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
         American Revolution
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
         Industrial Revolution
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">
         Great Divergence
        </a>
        . It took all of human history up to 1804 for the world's population to reach 1 billion; the next billion came just over a century later, in 1927.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-6">
          [6]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_history" title="Contemporary history">
         Contemporary history
        </a>
        is the span of historic events from approximately 1945 that are immediately relevant to the present time.
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Study_guide">
        Study guide
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Study guide">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Some events, while not without precedent, show a new way of perceiving the world. The concept of
       <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">
        modernity
       </a>
       interprets the general meaning of these events and seeks explanations for major developments.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Source_text">
        Source text
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Source text">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Historical_method" title="Historical method">
        Historical method
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Source_text" title="Source text">
        Source text
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The fundamental difficulty of studying modern history is the fact that a plethora of it has been documented up to the present day. It is imperative to consider the reliability of the information obtained from these records.
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
        Historiography
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">
        Philosophy of history
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_usage">
        Terminology and usage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Terminology and usage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-modern">
        Pre-modern
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pre-modern">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">
        Ancient history
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">
        Post-classical history
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the pre-modern era, many people's sense of self and purpose was often expressed via a
       <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">
        faith
       </a>
       in some form of
       <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
        deity
       </a>
       , be it that in a single
       <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
        God
       </a>
       or in many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gods" title="Gods">
        gods
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pre-modern cultures have not been thought of creating a sense of distinct individuality,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though. Religious officials, who often held positions of power, were the spiritual intermediaries to the common person. It was only through these intermediaries that the general masses had access to the
       <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">
        divine
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">
        Tradition
       </a>
       was
       <a href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">
        sacred
       </a>
       to ancient cultures and was unchanging and the
       <a href="/wiki/Social_order" title="Social order">
        social order
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Ceremony" title="Ceremony">
        ceremony
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morals" title="Morals">
        morals
       </a>
       in a culture could be strictly enforced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">
        Modern
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        modern
       </i>
       was coined in the 16th century to indicate present or recent times (ultimately derived from the Latin adverb
       <i>
        modo
       </i>
       , meaning "just now").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
        European Renaissance
       </a>
       (about 1420–1630), which marked the transition between the
       <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">
        Late Middle Ages
       </a>
       and Early Modern times, started in Italy and was spurred in part by the rediscovery of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">
        classical
       </a>
       art and literature, as well as the new perspectives gained from the
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">
        Age of Discovery
       </a>
       and the invention of the
       <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">
        telescope
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">
        microscope
       </a>
       , expanding the borders of thought and knowledge.
      </p>
      <p>
       In contrast to the pre-modern era, Western civilization made a
       <a href="#Western_transformations">
        gradual transition
       </a>
       from pre-modernity to modernity when
       <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">
        scientific methods
       </a>
       were developed which led many to believe that the use of science would lead to all knowledge, thus throwing back the shroud of
       <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">
        myth
       </a>
       under which pre-modern peoples lived. New information about the world was discovered via
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical_observation" title="Empirical observation">
        empirical observation
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       versus the historic use of
       <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">
        reason
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innate_knowledge" title="Innate knowledge">
        innate knowledge
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The term "Early Modern" was introduced in the English language in the 1930s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to distinguish the time between what we call Middle Ages and time of the late Enlightenment (1800) (when the meaning of the term Modern Ages was developing its contemporary form). It is important to note that these terms stem from European history. In usage in other parts of the world, such as in Asia, and in Muslim countries, the terms are applied in a very different way, but often in the context with their contact with European culture in the Age of Discovery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">
        Contemporary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Contemporary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_history" title="Contemporary history">
        Contemporary history
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the Contemporary era, there were various socio-technological trends. Regarding the 21st century and the late modern world, the Information Age and computers were forefront in use, not completely ubiquitous but often present in everyday life. The development of Eastern powers was of note, with China and India becoming more powerful. In the Eurasian theater, the European Union and Russian Federation were two forces recently developed. A concern for Western world, if not the whole world, was the late modern form of terrorism and the warfare that has resulted from the contemporary terrorist acts.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">
        Modern era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Significant_developments">
        Significant developments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Significant developments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The modern period has been a period of significant development in the fields of
       <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
        science
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">
        politics
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">
        warfare
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">
        technology
       </a>
       . It has also been an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_discovery" title="Age of discovery">
        age of discovery
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">
        globalization
       </a>
       . During this time, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(international)" title="Power (international)">
        European powers
       </a>
       and later their colonies, began a political, economic, and cultural
       <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">
        colonization
       </a>
       of the rest of the world.
      </p>
      <p>
       By the late 19th and 20th centuries,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist" title="Modernist">
        modernist
       </a>
       art, politics, science and culture has come to dominate not only
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">
        Western Europe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
        North America
       </a>
       , but almost every civilized area on the globe, including movements thought of as opposed to the west and globalization. The modern era is closely associated with the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">
        individualism
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCEref_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NCEref-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">
        capitalism
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
        urbanization
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCEref_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NCEref-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a belief in the possibilities of technological and political
       <a href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" title="Progress (history)">
        progress
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Wars and other perceived problems of this era, many of which come from the effects of rapid change, and the connected loss of strength of traditional religious and ethical norms, have led to many reactions against modern development.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Optimism and belief in constant progress has been most recently criticized by
       <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">
        postmodernism
       </a>
       while the dominance of Western Europe and
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-America" title="Anglo-America">
        Anglo-America
       </a>
       over other continents has been criticized by
       <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">
        postcolonial theory
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       One common conception of modernity is the condition of Western history since the mid-15th century, or roughly the European development of
       <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">
        movable type
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
        printing press
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this context the "modern" society is said to develop over many periods, and to be influenced by important events that represent breaks in the continuity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early">
        Early
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">
        Early modern period
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The modern era includes the early period, called the early modern period, which lasted from c. 1500 to around c. 1800 (most often 1815). Particular facets of early modernity include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
         Renaissance
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_reformation" title="Protestant reformation">
         Reformation
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counter_Reformation" title="Counter Reformation">
         Counter Reformation
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">
         Age of Discovery
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Rise of
        <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">
         capitalism
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Piracy" title="Golden Age of Piracy">
         Golden Age of Piracy
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Important events in the early modern period include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The invention of the
        <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">
         printing press
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
         English Civil War
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">
         Seven Years' War
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin:0 0 1em 1em; width:222px; text-align:left; clear:right;">
       <tr>
        <td>
         <div style="background:#f8eaba; text-align:center;">
          Modern Age characteristics
         </div>
         <p>
          The concept of the modern world as distinct from an ancient or medieval world rests on a sense that the modern world is not just another
          <a href="/wiki/Era" title="Era">
           era
          </a>
          in history, but rather the result of a new type of change. This is usually conceived of as progress driven by deliberate human efforts to better their situation.
         </p>
         <p>
          Advances in all areas of human activity—
          <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">
           politics
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">
           industry
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">
           society
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">
           economics
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">
           commerce
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">
           transport
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">
           communication
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">
           mechanization
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">
           automation
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
           science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">
           medicine
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">
           technology
          </a>
          , and
          <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">
           culture
          </a>
          —appear to have transformed an
          <i>
           Old World
          </i>
          into the
          <i>
           Modern
          </i>
          or
          <i>
           New World
          </i>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrosvenorModern_30-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-GrosvenorModern-30">
            [30]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicheletAndSimpson_31-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-MicheletAndSimpson-31">
            [31]
           </a>
          </sup>
          In each case, the identification of the old Revolutionary change can be used to demarcate the old and old-fashioned from the modern.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrosvenorModern_30-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-GrosvenorModern-30">
            [30]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicheletAndSimpson_31-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-MicheletAndSimpson-31">
            [31]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </p>
         <p>
          Portions of the Modern world altered its relationship with the
          <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
           Biblical
          </a>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_system" title="Value system">
           value system
          </a>
          , revalued the
          <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">
           monarchical
          </a>
          government system, and abolished the
          <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">
           feudal
          </a>
          economic system, with new
          <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">
           democratic
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">
           liberal
          </a>
          ideas in the areas of
          <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">
           politics
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
           science
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">
           psychology
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">
           sociology
          </a>
          , and
          <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">
           economics
          </a>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrosvenorModern_30-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-GrosvenorModern-30">
            [30]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicheletAndSimpson_31-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-MicheletAndSimpson-31">
            [31]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       This combination of epoch events totally changed thinking and thought in the early modern period, and so their dates serve as well as any to separate the old from the new modes.
      </p>
      <p>
       As an
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Revolution" title="Age of Revolution">
        Age of Revolutions
       </a>
       dawned, beginning with those revolts in
       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
        America
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        France
       </a>
       , political changes were then pushed forward in other countries partly as a result of upheavals of the
       <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
        Napoleonic Wars
       </a>
       and their impact on thought and thinking, from concepts from nationalism to organizing armies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The early period ended in a time of political and economic change as a result of
       <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">
        mechanization
       </a>
       in society, the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
        American Revolution
       </a>
       , the first
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
       ; other factors included the redrawing of the map of Europe by the
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Final_Act_of_the_Congress_of_Vienna" title="s:Final Act of the Congress of Vienna">
        Final Act
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">
        Congress of Vienna
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the peace established by
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1815)" title="Treaty of Paris (1815)">
        Second Treaty of Paris
       </a>
       which ended the Napoleonic Wars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Late">
        Late
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Late">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       As a result of the Industrial Revolution and the earlier political revolutions, the worldviews of
       <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">
        Modernism
       </a>
       emerged. The industrialization of many nations was initiated with the industrialization of Britain. Particular facets of the late modernity period include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Increasing role of
        <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
         science
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">
         technology
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Mass
        <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">
         literacy
        </a>
        and proliferation of
        <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">
         mass media
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Spread of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_movements" title="Social movements">
         social movements
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Institution of
        <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">
         representative democracy
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">
         Individualism
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">
         Industrialization
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
         Urbanization
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">
         Total fertility rates
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Ecological_collapse" title="Ecological collapse">
         ecological collapses
        </a>
        occurring at
        <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">
         geometric rates
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Other important events in the development of the Late modern period include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
         American Revolution
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
         French Revolution
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">
         Revolutions of 1848
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_(1917)" title="Russian Revolution (1917)">
         Russian Revolution
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
         First World War
        </a>
        and the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
         Second World War
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Our most recent era—Modern Times—begins with the end of these revolutions in the 19th century,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and includes the World Wars era
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (encompassing
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       ) and the emergence of
       <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">
        socialist countries
       </a>
       that led to the
       <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
        Cold War
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        contemporary era
       </i>
       follows shortly afterward with the explosion of research and increase of knowledge known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Information_Age" title="Information Age">
        Information Age
       </a>
       in the latter 20th and the early 21st century. Today's
       <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">
        Postmodern
       </a>
       era is seen in widespread
       <a href="/wiki/Digitality" title="Digitality">
        digitality
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_period">
        Early modern period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early modern period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">
        Early modern period
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7590" data-file-width="13708" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/550px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/825px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/1100px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 2x" width="550"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemüller map">
            Waldseemüller map
           </a>
           with joint sheets, 1507
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>
      <p>
       Historians consider the early modern period to be approximately between 1500 and 1800. It follows the Late Middle Ages period and is marked by the first European colonies, the rise of strong centralized governments, and the beginnings of recognizable nation-states that are the direct antecedents of today's states.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Africa and the Ottoman Empire, the Muslim expansion took place in North and East Africa. In West Africa, various native nations existed. The Indian Empires and civilizations of Southeast Asia were a vital link in the spice trade. On the Indian subcontinent, the Great Mughal Empire existed. The archipelagic empires, the Sultanate of Malacca and later the Sultanate of Johor, controlled the southern areas.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Asia, various Chinese dynasties and Japanese shogunates controlled the Asian sphere. In Japan, the Edo period from 1600 to 1868 is also referred to as the early modern period. And in Korea, from the rising of Joseon Dynasty to the enthronement of King Gojong is referred to as the early modern period. In the Americas, Native Americans had built a large and varied civilization, including the Aztec Empire and alliance, the Inca civilization, the Mayan Empire and cities, and the Chibcha Confederation. In the west, the European kingdoms and movements were in a movement of reformation and expansion. Russia reached the Pacific coast in 1647 and consolidated its control over the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">
        Russian Far East
       </a>
       in the 19th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       Later religious trends of the period saw the end of the expansion of Muslims and the Muslim world. Christians and Christendom saw the end of the Crusades and end of religious unity under the Roman Catholic Church. It was during this time that the Inquisitions and Protestant reformations took place.
      </p>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg/750px-Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg/1125px-Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg/1500px-Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg.png 2x" width="750"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_empires_in_1800.svg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
           Colonial empires in 1800
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the early modern period, an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_discovery" title="Age of discovery">
        age of discovery
       </a>
       and trade was undertaken by the Western European nations. Portugal, Spain, the Netherlands, the United Kingdom and France went on a colonial expansion and took possession of lands and set up colonies in Africa, southern Asia, and North and South America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Turkey colonized Southeastern Europe, and parts of the West Asia and North Africa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Russia took possession in Eastern Europe, Asia, and North America.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">
        Asia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Asia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="China">
        China
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: China">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">
        Qing dynasty
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In China, urbanization increased as the population grew and as the division of labor grew more complex. Large urban centers, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">
        Nanjing
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       , also contributed to the growth of private industry. In particular, small-scale industries grew up, often specializing in paper, silk, cotton, and porcelain goods. For the most part, however, relatively small urban centers with markets proliferated around the country. Town markets mainly traded food, with some necessary manufactures such as pins or oil. Despite the
       <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">
        xenophobia
       </a>
       and intellectual introspection characteristic of the increasingly popular new school of
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucianism" title="Neo-Confucianism">
        neo-Confucianism
       </a>
       , China under the early
       <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">
        Ming dynasty
       </a>
       was not isolated. Foreign trade and other contacts with the outside world, particularly Japan, increased considerably. Chinese merchants explored all of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
        Indian Ocean
       </a>
       , reaching East Africa with the
       <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">
        treasure voyages
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">
        Zheng He
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">
        Qing dynasty
       </a>
       (1644–1912) was founded after the fall of the Ming, the last
       <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">
        Han Chinese
       </a>
       dynasty, by the
       <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">
        Manchus
       </a>
       . The Manchus were formerly known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">
        Jurchens
       </a>
       . When Beijing was captured by
       <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">
        Li Zicheng
       </a>
       's peasant rebels in 1644, the
       <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">
        Chongzhen Emperor
       </a>
       , the last Ming emperor, committed suicide. The Manchus then allied with former Ming general
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">
        Wu Sangui
       </a>
       and seized control of
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       , which became the new capital of the Qing dynasty. The Manchus adopted the Confucian norms of traditional Chinese government in their rule of
       <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">
        China proper
       </a>
       . Schoppa, the editor of
       <i>
        The Columbia Guide to Modern Chinese History
       </i>
       argues, "A date around 1780 as the beginning of modern China is thus closer to what we know today as historical 'reality'. It also allows us to have a better baseline to understand the precipitous decline of the Chinese polity in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">
        Japan
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Japan">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">
        Tokugawa shogunate
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In pre-modern
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Japan following the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sengoku_Period" title="Sengoku Period">
        Sengoku Period
       </a>
       of "warring states", central government had been largely reestablished by
       <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">
        Oda Nobunaga
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">
        Toyotomi Hideyoshi
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Azuchi%E2%80%93Momoyama_period" title="Azuchi–Momoyama period">
        Azuchi–Momoyama period
       </a>
       . After the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sekigahara" title="Battle of Sekigahara">
        Battle of Sekigahara
       </a>
       in 1600, central authority fell to
       <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">
        Tokugawa Ieyasu
       </a>
       who completed this process and received the title of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">
         shogun
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1603.
      </p>
      <p>
       Society in the Japanese "
       <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">
        Tokugawa period
       </a>
       " (
       <a href="/wiki/Edo_society" title="Edo society">
        Edo society
       </a>
       ), unlike the shogunates before it, was based on the strict class
       <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">
        hierarchy
       </a>
       originally established by
       <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">
        Toyotomi Hideyoshi
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Daimy%C5%8D" title="Daimyō">
         daimyōs
        </a>
       </i>
       (feudal lords) were at the top, followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Warrior" title="Warrior">
        warrior
       </a>
       -caste of
       <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">
        samurai
       </a>
       , with the
       <a href="/wiki/Farmer" title="Farmer">
        farmers
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artisans" title="Artisans">
        artisans
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">
        traders
       </a>
       ranking below. In some parts of the country, particularly smaller regions,
       <i>
        daimyōs
       </i>
       and samurai were more or less identical, since
       <i>
        daimyōs
       </i>
       might be trained as samurai, and samurai might act as local lords. Otherwise, the largely inflexible nature of this
       <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">
        social stratification
       </a>
       system unleashed disruptive forces over time.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxes" title="Taxes">
        Taxes
       </a>
       on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peasantry" title="Peasantry">
        peasantry
       </a>
       were set at fixed amounts which did not account for
       <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
        inflation
       </a>
       or other changes in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary" title="Monetary">
        monetary
       </a>
       value. As a result, the tax revenues collected by the samurai landowners were worth less and less over time. This often led to numerous confrontations between noble but impoverished samurai and well-to-do peasants, ranging from simple local disturbances to much bigger
       <a href="/wiki/Rebellion" title="Rebellion">
        rebellions
       </a>
       . None, however, proved compelling enough to seriously challenge the established order until the arrival of foreign powers.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="India">
        India
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: India">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">
        Mughal Empire
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">
        Maratha Empire
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       On the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">
        Indian subcontinent
       </a>
       , the Mughal Empire ruled most of India in the early 18th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The "classic period" ended with the death and defeat of Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">
        Aurangzeb
       </a>
       in 1707 by the rising
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">
        Hindu
       </a>
       Maratha Empire,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although the dynasty continued for another 150 years. During this period, the Empire was marked by a highly centralized administration connecting the different regions. All the significant monuments of the Mughals, their most visible legacy, date to this period which was characterised by the expansion of Persian cultural influence in the Indian subcontinent, with brilliant literary, artistic, and architectural results. The Maratha Empire was located in the south west of present-day India and expanded greatly under the rule of the
       <a href="/wiki/Peshwa" title="Peshwa">
        Peshwas
       </a>
       , the prime ministers of the Maratha empire. In 1761, the Maratha army lost the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Battle_of_Panipat" title="Third Battle of Panipat">
        Third Battle of Panipat
       </a>
       which halted imperial expansion and the empire was then divided into a confederacy of Maratha states.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="British_and_Dutch_colonization">
        British and Dutch colonization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: British and Dutch colonization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The development of
       <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">
        New Imperialism
       </a>
       saw the conquest of nearly all eastern hemisphere territories by colonial powers. The
       <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">
        commercial colonization of India
       </a>
       commenced in 1757, after the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">
        Battle of Plassey
       </a>
       , when the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Bengal" title="Nawab of Bengal">
        Nawab of Bengal
       </a>
       surrendered his dominions to the British East India Company,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in 1765, when the Company was granted the
       <i>
        diwani
       </i>
       , or the right to collect revenue, in
       <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">
        Bengal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">
        Bihar
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or in 1772, when the Company established a capital in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">
        Calcutta
       </a>
       , appointed its first
       <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_India" title="Governor-General of India">
        Governor-General
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Warren_Hastings" title="Warren Hastings">
        Warren Hastings
       </a>
       , and became directly involved in governance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">
        Maratha states
       </a>
       , following the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Maratha_wars" title="Anglo-Maratha wars">
        Anglo-Maratha wars
       </a>
       , eventually lost to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">
        British East India Company
       </a>
       in 1818 with the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Anglo-Maratha_War" title="Third Anglo-Maratha War">
        Third Anglo-Maratha War
       </a>
       . The rule lasted until 1858, when, after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian rebellion of 1857">
        Indian rebellion of 1857
       </a>
       and consequent of the
       <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_1858" title="Government of India Act 1858">
        Government of India Act 1858
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/India_Office" title="India Office">
        British government
       </a>
       assumed the task of directly administering India in the new
       <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">
        British Raj
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WDL-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1819
       <a href="/wiki/Stamford_Raffles" title="Stamford Raffles">
        Stamford Raffles
       </a>
       established
       <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">
        Singapore
       </a>
       as a key trading post for Britain in their rivalry with the Dutch. However, their rivalry cooled in 1824 when an
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Dutch_Treaty_of_1824" title="Anglo-Dutch Treaty of 1824">
        Anglo-Dutch treaty
       </a>
       demarcated their respective interests in Southeast Asia. From the 1850s onwards, the pace of colonization shifted to a significantly higher gear.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">
        Dutch East India Company
       </a>
       (1800) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">
        British East India Company
       </a>
       (1858) were dissolved by their respective governments, who took over the direct administration of the colonies. Only
       <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
        Thailand
       </a>
       was spared the experience of foreign rule, although, Thailand itself was also greatly affected by the power politics of the Western powers. Colonial rule had a profound effect on Southeast Asia. While the colonial powers profited much from the region's vast resources and large market, colonial rule did develop the region to a varying extent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">
        Europe
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Europe">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Many major events caused
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       to change around the start of the 16th century, starting with the
       <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">
        Fall of Constantinople
       </a>
       in 1453, the fall of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_Spain" title="Muslim Spain">
        Muslim Spain
       </a>
       and the discovery of the
       <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">
        Americas
       </a>
       in 1492, and
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">
        Martin Luther
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestant Reformation
       </a>
       in 1517. In
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       the modern period is often dated to the start of the
       <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">
        Tudor period
       </a>
       with the victory of
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">
        Henry VII
       </a>
       over
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">
        Richard III
       </a>
       at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth" title="Battle of Bosworth">
        Battle of Bosworth
       </a>
       in 1485.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">
        Early modern European
       </a>
       history is usually seen to span from the start of the 15th century, through the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Reason" title="Age of Reason">
        Age of Reason
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">
        Age of Enlightenment
       </a>
       in the 17th and 18th centuries, until the beginning of the Industrial Revolution in the late 18th century.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tsardom_of_Russia">
        Tsardom of Russia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tsardom of Russia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">
        Tsardom of Russia
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russparis.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1602" data-file-width="2500" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Russparis.jpg/300px-Russparis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Russparis.jpg/450px-Russparis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Russparis.jpg/600px-Russparis.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russparis.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Imperial Russian Cossacks (left) in Paris in 1814
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Russia experienced territorial growth through the 17th century, which was the age of
       <a href="/wiki/Cossacks" title="Cossacks">
        Cossacks
       </a>
       . Cossacks were warriors organized into military communities, resembling
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirates" title="Pirates">
        pirates
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pioneers_of_the_New_World" title="Pioneers of the New World">
        pioneers of the New World
       </a>
       . The native land of the Cossacks is defined by a line of Russian/Ruthenian town-fortresses located on the border with the
       <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">
        steppe
       </a>
       and stretching from the middle Volga to Ryazan and Tula, then breaking abruptly to the south and extending to the Dnieper via Pereyaslavl. This area was settled by a population of free people practicing various trades and crafts.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1648, the peasants of
       <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
        Ukraine
       </a>
       joined the
       <a href="/wiki/Zaporozhian_Cossacks" title="Zaporozhian Cossacks">
        Zaporozhian Cossacks
       </a>
       in rebellion against Poland-Lithuania during the
       <a href="/wiki/Khmelnytsky_Uprising" title="Khmelnytsky Uprising">
        Khmelnytsky Uprising
       </a>
       , because of the social and religious oppression they suffered under Polish rule. In 1654 the Ukrainian leader,
       <a href="/wiki/Bohdan_Khmelnytsky" title="Bohdan Khmelnytsky">
        Bohdan Khmelnytsky
       </a>
       , offered to place Ukraine under the protection of the Russian Tsar,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksey_I" title="Aleksey I">
        Aleksey I
       </a>
       . Aleksey's acceptance of this offer led to another
       <a href="/wiki/Russo-Polish_War_(1654%E2%80%931667)" title="Russo-Polish War (1654–1667)">
        Russo-Polish War (1654–1667)
       </a>
       . Finally, Ukraine was split along the river
       <a href="/wiki/Dnieper" title="Dnieper">
        Dnieper
       </a>
       , leaving the western part (or
       <a href="/wiki/Right-bank_Ukraine" title="Right-bank Ukraine">
        Right-bank Ukraine
       </a>
       ) under Polish rule and eastern part (
       <a href="/wiki/Left-bank_Ukraine" title="Left-bank Ukraine">
        Left-bank Ukraine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">
        Kiev
       </a>
       ) under Russian. Later, in 1670–71 the
       <a href="/wiki/Don_Cossacks" title="Don Cossacks">
        Don Cossacks
       </a>
       led by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stenka_Razin" title="Stenka Razin">
        Stenka Razin
       </a>
       initiated a major uprising in the Volga region, but the Tsar's troops were successful in defeating the rebels. In the east, the rapid Russian exploration and colonisation of the huge territories of Siberia was led mostly by Cossacks hunting for valuable
       <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">
        furs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">
        ivory
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_explorers" title="Russian explorers">
        Russian explorers
       </a>
       pushed eastward primarily along the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siberian_river_routes" title="Siberian river routes">
        Siberian river routes
       </a>
       , and by the mid-17th century there were Russian settlements in the Eastern Siberia, on the
       <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Peninsula" title="Chukchi Peninsula">
        Chukchi Peninsula
       </a>
       , along the
       <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">
        Amur River
       </a>
       , and on the Pacific coast. In 1648 the
       <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">
        Bering Strait
       </a>
       between Asia and North America was passed for the first time by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fedot_Popov" title="Fedot Popov">
        Fedot Popov
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semyon_Dezhnyov" title="Semyon Dezhnyov">
        Semyon Dezhnyov
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_and_Enlightenment">
        Reason and Enlightenment
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reason and Enlightenment">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">
        Great Divergence
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Traditionally, the European intellectual transformation of and after the Renaissance bridged the Middle Ages and the Modern era. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Reason" title="Age of Reason">
        Age of Reason
       </a>
       in the Western world is generally regarded as being the start of
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">
        modern philosophy
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a departure from the medieval approach, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">
        Scholasticism
       </a>
       . Early 17th-century philosophy is often called the Age of Rationalism and is considered to succeed Renaissance philosophy and precede the Age of Enlightenment, but some consider it as the earliest part of the Enlightenment era in philosophy, extending that era to two centuries. The 18th century saw the beginning of
       <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">
        secularization
       </a>
       in Europe, rising to notability in the wake of the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">
        Age of Enlightenment
       </a>
       is a time in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">
        Western philosophy
       </a>
       and cultural life centered upon the 18th century in which reason was advocated as the primary source and legitimacy for authority. Enlightenment gained momentum more or less simultaneously in many parts of Europe and America. Developing during the Enlightenment era,
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">
        Renaissance humanism
       </a>
       as an intellectual movement spread across Europe. The basic training of the humanist was to speak well and write (typically, in the form of a letter). The term
       <i>
        umanista
       </i>
       comes from the latter part of the 15th century. The people were associated with the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studia_humanitatis" title="Studia humanitatis">
         studia humanitatis
        </a>
       </i>
       , a novel curriculum that was competing with the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Quadrivium" title="Quadrivium">
         quadrivium
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholastic_logic" title="Scholastic logic">
        scholastic logic
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Renaissance humanism took a close study of the Latin and Greek classical texts, and was antagonistic to the values of
       <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">
        scholasticism
       </a>
       with its emphasis on the accumulated commentaries; and humanists were involved in the sciences, philosophies, arts and poetry of classical antiquity. They self-consciously imitated
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        classical Latin
       </a>
       and deprecated the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">
        medieval Latin
       </a>
       . By analogy with the perceived decline of Latin, they applied the principle of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ad_fontes" title="Ad fontes">
         ad fontes
        </a>
       </i>
       , or back to the sources, across broad areas of learning.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Quarrel_of_the_Ancients_and_the_Moderns" title="Quarrel of the Ancients and the Moderns">
        quarrel of the Ancients and the Moderns
       </a>
       was a
       <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">
        literary
       </a>
       and artistic quarrel that heated up in the early 1690s and shook the
       <a href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_fran%C3%A7aise" title="Académie française">
        Académie française
       </a>
       . The opposing two sides were, the Ancients (
       <i>
        Anciens
       </i>
       ) who constrain choice of subjects to those drawn from the literature of
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">
        Antiquity
       </a>
       and the Moderns (
       <i>
        Modernes
       </i>
       ), who supported the merits of the authors of the century of
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">
        Louis XIV
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernard_le_Bovier_de_Fontenelle" title="Bernard le Bovier de Fontenelle">
        Fontenelle
       </a>
       quickly followed with his
       <i>
        Digression sur les anciens et les modernes
       </i>
       (1688), in which he took the Modern side, pressing the argument that modern scholarship allowed modern man to surpass the ancients in knowledge.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_Revolution">
        Scientific Revolution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Scientific Revolution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">
        Scientific Revolution
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Scientific Revolution was a period when European ideas in
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
        classical physics
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_astronomy" title="Classical astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_biology" title="Classical biology">
        biology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy#Early_modern_anatomy" title="History of anatomy">
        human anatomy
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_chemistry" title="Classical chemistry">
        chemistry
       </a>
       , and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_science" title="Classical science">
        classical sciences
       </a>
       were rejected and led to doctrines supplanting those that had prevailed from
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        Ancient Greece
       </a>
       to the Middle Ages which would lead to a transition to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_science" title="Modern science">
        modern science
       </a>
       . This period saw a fundamental transformation in scientific ideas across
       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
        physics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
        biology
       </a>
       , in institutions supporting
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_investigation" title="Scientific investigation">
        scientific investigation
       </a>
       , and in the more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worldview" title="Worldview">
        widely held picture of the universe
       </a>
       . Individuals started to question all manners of things and it was this questioning that led to the Scientific Revolution, which in turn formed the foundations of contemporary sciences and the establishment of several modern scientific fields.
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_electromagnetism" title="History of electromagnetism">
        History of electromagnetism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Science in the Age of Enlightenment">
        Science in the Age of Enlightenment
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_French_Revolutions">
        The French Revolutions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The French Revolutions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Toward the middle and latter stages of the Age of Revolution, the French political and social revolutions and radical change saw the French governmental structure, previously an absolute monarchy with feudal privileges for the aristocracy and Catholic clergy transform, changing to forms based on Enlightenment principles of citizenship and inalienable rights. The first revolution led to government by the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(French_Revolution)" title="National Assembly (French Revolution)">
        National Assembly
       </a>
       , the second by the
       <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_(France)" title="Legislative Assembly (France)">
        Legislative Assembly
       </a>
       , and the third by the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Directory" title="French Directory">
        Directory
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The changes were accompanied by violent turmoil which included the trial and execution of the king, vast bloodshed and repression during the Reign of Terror, and warfare involving every other major European power. Subsequent events that can be traced to the Revolution include the Napoleonic Wars, two separate restorations of the monarchy, and two additional revolutions as modern France took shape. In the following century, France would be governed at one point or another as a republic, constitutional monarchy, and two different empires.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="National_and_Legislative_Assembly">
        National and Legislative Assembly
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: National and Legislative Assembly">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(French_Revolution)" title="National Assembly (French Revolution)">
        National Assembly (French Revolution)
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_(France)" title="Legislative Assembly (France)">
        Legislative Assembly (France)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the French Revolution, the National Assembly, which existed from June 17 to July 9 of 1789, was a transitional body between the Estates-General and the National Constituent Assembly.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Legislative Assembly was the legislature of France from October 1, 1791 to September 1792. It provided the focus of political debate and revolutionary law-making between the periods of the National Constituent Assembly and of the National Convention.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Directory_and_Napoleonic_Era">
        The Directory and Napoleonic Era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: The Directory and Napoleonic Era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/French_Directory" title="French Directory">
        French Directory
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Era" title="Napoleonic Era">
        Napoleonic Era
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Executive Directory was a body of five Directors that held executive power in France following the Convention and preceding the Consulate. The period of this regime (2 November 1795 until 10 November 1799), commonly known as the Directory (or Directoire) era, constitutes the second to last stage of the French Revolution.
