Napoleon, before seizing the title of Emperor, was elected as First Consul of the Consulate of France.
      </p>
      <p>
       The campaigns of French Emperor and General Napoleon Bonaparte characterized the Napoleonic Era. Born on Corsica as the French invaded, and dying suspiciously on the tiny British Island of St. Helena, this brilliant commander, controlled a French Empire that, at its height, ruled a large portion of Europe directly from Paris, while many of his friends and family ruled countries such as Spain, Poland, several parts of Italy and many other Kingdoms Republics and dependencies. The Napoleonic Era changed the face of Europe forever, and old Empires and Kingdoms fell apart as a result of the mighty and "Glorious" surge of Republicanism.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_unification">
        Italian unification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Italian unification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_unification" title="Italian unification">
        Italian unification
       </a>
       was the political and social movement that annexed different states of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">
        Italian peninsula
       </a>
       into the single state of Italy in the 19th century. There is a lack of consensus on the exact dates for the beginning and the end of this period, but many scholars agree that the process began with the end of Napoleonic rule and the
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">
        Congress of Vienna
       </a>
       in 1815, and approximately ended with the
       <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">
        Franco-Prussian War
       </a>
       in 1871, though the last
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italia_irredenta" title="Italia irredenta">
         città irredente
        </a>
       </i>
       did not join the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">
        Kingdom of Italy
       </a>
       until after World War I.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_early_modern_period">
        End of the early modern period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: End of the early modern period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Toward the end of the early modern period, Europe was dominated by the evolving system of mercantile capitalism in its trade and the New Economy. European states and politics had the characteristic of Absolutism. The French power and English revolutions dominated the political scene. There eventually evolved an international balance of power that held at bay a great conflagration until years later.
      </p>
      <p>
       The end date of the early modern period is usually associated with the Industrial Revolution, which began in Britain in about 1750. Another significant date is 1789, the beginning of the French Revolution, which drastically transformed the state of European politics and ushered in the Prince Edward Era and modern Europe.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">
        North America
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: North America">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_Wars" title="French and Indian Wars">
        French and Indian Wars
       </a>
       were a series of conflicts in North America that represented the actions there that accompanied the European dynastic wars. In Quebec, the wars are generally referred to as the Intercolonial Wars. While some conflicts involved Spanish and Dutch forces, all pitted Great Britain, its colonies and American Indian allies on one side and France, its colonies and Indian allies on the other.
      </p>
      <p>
       The expanding French and British colonies were contending for control of the western, or interior, territories. Whenever the European countries went to war, there were actions within and by these colonies although the dates of the conflict did not necessarily exactly coincide with those of the larger conflicts.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1969" data-file-width="2976" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/220px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/330px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg/440px-Declaration_of_Independence_%281819%29%2C_by_John_Trumbull.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Declaration_of_Independence_(1819),_by_John_Trumbull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">
          John Trumbull
         </a>
         's
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trumbull%27s_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Trumbull's Declaration of Independence">
           Declaration of Independence
          </a>
         </i>
         , showing the five-man committee in charge of drafting the Declaration in 1776 as it presents its work to the
         <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">
          Second Continental Congress
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">
          Philadelphia
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Beginning the Age of Revolution, the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">
        American Revolution
       </a>
       and the ensuing political upheaval during the last half of the 18th century saw the Thirteen Colonies of North America overthrow the governance of the Parliament of Great Britain, and then reject the British monarchy itself to become the sovereign United States of America. In this period the colonies first rejected the authority of the Parliament to govern them without representation, and formed self-governing independent states. The Second Continental Congress then joined together against the British to defend that self-governance in the armed conflict from 1775 to 1783 known as the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">
        American Revolutionary War
       </a>
       (also called American War of Independence).
      </p>
      <p>
       The American Revolution began with fighting at Lexington and Concord. On July 4, 1776, they issued the Declaration of Independence, which proclaimed their independence from Great Britain and their formation of a cooperative union. In June 1776,
       <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">
        Benjamin Franklin
       </a>
       was appointed a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">
        Committee of Five
       </a>
       that drafted the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">
        Declaration of Independence
       </a>
       . Although he was temporarily disabled by gout and unable to attend most meetings of the Committee, Franklin made several small changes to the draft sent to him by
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">
        Thomas Jefferson
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The rebellious states defeated Great Britain in the American Revolutionary War, the first successful colonial war of independence. While the states had already rejected the governance of Parliament, through the Declaration the new United States now rejected the legitimacy of the monarchy to demand allegiance. The war raged for seven years, with effective American victory, followed by formal British abandonment of any claim to the United States with the Treaty of Paris.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census,_1909.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="996" data-file-width="1174" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg/220px-North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg/330px-North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg/440px-North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census%2C_1909.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_America_1797_-_U.S._Bureau_of_the_Census,_1909.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         North America 1797
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" title="Philadelphia Convention">
        Philadelphia Convention
       </a>
       set up the current United States; the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">
        United States Constitution
       </a>
       ratification the following year made the states part of a single republic with a limited central government. The
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">
        Bill of Rights
       </a>
       , comprising ten constitutional amendments guaranteeing many fundamental civil rights and freedoms, was ratified in 1791.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Decolonization_of_North_and_South_Americas">
        Decolonization of North and South Americas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Decolonization of North and South Americas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_the_Americas" title="Decolonization of the Americas">
        Decolonization of the Americas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">
        Spanish American wars of independence
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The decolonization of the Americas was the process by which the countries in the Americas gained their independence from European rule. Decolonization began with a series of revolutions in the late 18th and early-to-mid-19th centuries. The Spanish American wars of independence were the numerous wars against Spanish rule in Spanish America that took place during the early 19th century, from 1808 until 1829, directly related to the Napoleonic French invasion of Spain. The conflict started with short-lived governing juntas established in Chuquisaca and Quito opposing the composition of the Supreme Central Junta of Seville.
      </p>
      <table class="toccolours" style="margin:auto;">
       <tr>
        <td style="background:#f8eaba; text-align:center;">

           </dt>
          </dl>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

            </div>

          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       When the Central Junta fell to the French, numerous new Juntas appeared all across the Americas, eventually resulting in a chain of newly independent countries stretching from Argentina and Chile in the south, to Mexico in the north. After the death of the king Ferdinand VII, in 1833, only Cuba and Puerto Rico remained under Spanish rule, until the Spanish–American War in 1898. Unlike the Spanish, the Portuguese did not divide their colonial territory in America. The captaincies they created were subdued to a centralized administration in Salvador (later relocated to Rio de Janeiro) which reported directly to the Portuguese Crown until its independence in 1822, becoming the
       <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Brazil" title="Empire of Brazil">
        Empire of Brazil
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_wars_of_independence" title="Latin American wars of independence">
        Latin American wars of independence
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Timeline of the Spanish American wars of independence">
        Timeline of the Spanish American wars of independence
       </a>
      </div>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_revolutions">
        Industrial revolutions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Industrial revolutions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
        Industrial Revolution
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Industrial_Revolution" title="Second Industrial Revolution">
        Second Industrial Revolution
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="1024" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/220px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/330px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg/440px-Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquina_vapor_Watt_ETSIIM.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Watt_steam_engine" title="Watt steam engine">
          Watt steam engine
         </a>
         . The development of the
         <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">
          steam engine
         </a>
         started the Industrial Revolution in
         <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
          Great Britain
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
          <a href="#cite_note-55">
           [55]
          </a>
         </sup>
         The steam engine was created to pump water from coal mines, enabling them to be deepened beyond
         <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">
          groundwater
         </a>
         levels.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The date of the Industrial Revolution is not exact.
       <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm" title="Eric Hobsbawm">
        Eric Hobsbawm
       </a>
       held that it 'broke out' in the 1780s and was not fully felt until the 1830s or 1840s,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T.S._Ashton" title="T.S. Ashton">
        T.S. Ashton
       </a>
       held that it occurred roughly between 1760 and 1830 (in effect the reigns of
       <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">
        George III
       </a>
       , The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Regency" title="English Regency">
        Regency
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">
        George IV
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The great changes of centuries before the 19th were more connected with ideas, religion or military conquest, and technological advance had only made small changes in the material wealth of ordinary people.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first Industrial Revolution merged into the Second Industrial Revolution around 1850, when technological and economic progress gained momentum with the development of steam-powered ships and railways, and later in the 19th century with the
       <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">
        internal combustion engine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">
        electric power
       </a>
       generation. The Second Industrial Revolution was a phase of the Industrial Revolution; labeled as the separate Technical Revolution. From a technological and a social point of view there is no clean break between the two. Major innovations during the period occurred in the chemical, electrical, petroleum, and steel industries. Specific advancements included the introduction of oil fired steam turbine and internal combustion driven steel ships, the development of the airplane, the practical commercialization of the automobile, mass production of consumer goods, the perfection of canning, mechanical refrigeration and other food preservation techniques, and the invention of the telephone.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Industrialization">
        Industrialization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Industrialization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">
        Industrialization
       </a>
       is the process of social and economic change whereby a human group is transformed from a pre-industrial society into an industrial one. It is a subdivision of a more general
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernization" title="Modernization">
        modernization
       </a>
       process, where
       <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">
        social change
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">
        economic development
       </a>
       are closely related with
       <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">
        technological innovation
       </a>
       , particularly with the development of large-scale energy and metallurgy production. It is the extensive organization of an economy for the purpose of manufacturing. Industrialization also introduces a form of philosophical change, where people obtain a different attitude towards their
       <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">
        perception of nature
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution_in_manufacture_and_power">
        Revolution in manufacture and power
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Revolution in manufacture and power">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       An
       <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">
        economy
       </a>
       based on
       <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">
        manual labour
       </a>
       was replaced by one dominated by industry and the manufacture of machinery. It began with the mechanization of the
       <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">
        textile
       </a>
       industries and the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       -making techniques, and trade expansion was enabled by the introduction of
       <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">
        canals
       </a>
       , improved roads, and then
       <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">
        railways
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The introduction of
       <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">
        steam power
       </a>
       (fuelled primarily by
       <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
        coal
       </a>
       ) and powered machinery (mainly in
       <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">
        textile manufacturing
       </a>
       ) underpinned the dramatic increases in production capacity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The development of all-metal
       <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">
        machine tools
       </a>
       in the first two decades of the 19th century facilitated the manufacture of more production machines for manufacturing in other industries.
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern
       <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">
        petroleum industry
       </a>
       started in 1846 with the discovery of the process of refining
       <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">
        kerosene
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
        coal
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">
        Nova Scotian
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pineo_Gesner" title="Abraham Pineo Gesner">
        Abraham Pineo Gesner
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_%C5%81ukasiewicz" title="Ignacy Łukasiewicz">
        Ignacy Łukasiewicz
       </a>
       improved Gesner's method to develop a means of refining kerosene from the more readily available "rock oil" ("petr-oleum")
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seep" title="Seep">
        seeps
       </a>
       in 1852 and the first rock oil mine was built in
       <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B3brka,_Krosno_County" title="Bóbrka, Krosno County">
        Bóbrka
       </a>
       , near
       <a href="/wiki/Krosno" title="Krosno">
        Krosno
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galicia_(Central_Europe)" title="Galicia (Central Europe)">
        Galicia
       </a>
       in the following year. In 1854,
       <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Silliman" title="Benjamin Silliman">
        Benjamin Silliman
       </a>
       , a science professor at
       <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">
        Yale University
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/New_Haven,_Connecticut" title="New Haven, Connecticut">
        New Haven
       </a>
       , was the first to fractionate petroleum by distillation. These discoveries rapidly spread around the world.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_engineers">
        Notable engineers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notable engineers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Engineering achievements of the revolution ranged from electrification to developments in materials science. The advancements made a great contribution to the quality of life. In the first revolution,
       <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Paul" title="Lewis Paul">
        Lewis Paul
       </a>
       was the original inventor of roller spinning, the basis of the water frame for spinning cotton in a cotton mill.
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">
        Matthew Boulton
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">
        James Watt
       </a>
       's improvements to the steam engine were fundamental to the changes brought by the Industrial Revolution in both the Kingdom of Great Britain and the world.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Teslathinker.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1554" data-file-width="1206" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Teslathinker.jpg/220px-Teslathinker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Teslathinker.jpg/330px-Teslathinker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Teslathinker.jpg/440px-Teslathinker.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Teslathinker.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Nikola Tesla sits in front of the spiral coil of his high-frequency transformer at East Houston Street, New York.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the latter part of the second revolution,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Alva_Edison" title="Thomas Alva Edison">
        Thomas Alva Edison
       </a>
       developed many devices that greatly influenced life around the world and is often credited with the creation of the first industrial research laboratory. In 1882, Edison switched on the world's first large-scale
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_supply_network" title="Electrical supply network">
        electrical supply network
       </a>
       that provided 110 volts direct current to fifty-nine customers in lower Manhattan. Also toward the end of the second industrial revolution,
       <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">
        Nikola Tesla
       </a>
       made many contributions in the field of
       <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">
        electricity
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">
        magnetism
       </a>
       in the late 19th and early 20th centuries.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_effects_and_classes">
        Social effects and classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Social effects and classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Industrial Revolutions were major
       <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">
        technological
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">
        socioeconomic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">
        cultural
       </a>
       changes in late 18th and early 19th centuries that began in Britain and spread throughout the world. The effects spread throughout
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">
        Western Europe
       </a>
       and North America during the 19th century, eventually affecting the majority of the world. The impact of this change on
       <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">
        society
       </a>
       was enormous and is often compared to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_revolution" title="Neolithic revolution">
        Neolithic revolution
       </a>
       , when mankind developed
       <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
        agriculture
       </a>
       and gave up its
       <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">
        nomadic
       </a>
       lifestyle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It has been argued that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_Domestic_Product" title="Gross Domestic Product">
        GDP
       </a>
       per capita was much more stable and progressed at a much slower rate until the industrial revolution and the emergence of the modern
       <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">
        capitalist
       </a>
       economy, and that it has since increased rapidly in capitalist countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-19th-century_European_revolts">
        Mid-19th-century European revolts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Mid-19th-century European revolts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">
        European Revolutions of 1848
       </a>
       , known in some countries as the Spring of Nations or the Year of Revolution, were a series of political upheavals throughout the European continent. Described as a revolutionary wave, the period of unrest began in France and then, further propelled by the French Revolution of 1848, soon spread to the rest of Europe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although most of the revolutions were quickly put down, there was a significant amount of violence in many areas, with tens of thousands of people tortured and killed. While the immediate political effects of the revolutions were reversed, the long-term reverberations of the events were far-reaching.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_age_reformism">
        Industrial age reformism
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Industrial age reformism">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Industrial age
       <a href="/wiki/Reform_movement" title="Reform movement">
        reform movements
       </a>
       began the gradual change of society rather than with episodes of rapid fundamental changes. The reformists' ideas were often grounded in liberalism, although they also possessed aspects of utopian, socialist or religious concepts. The Radical movement campaigned for electoral reform, a reform of the Poor Laws, free trade, educational reform, postal reform, prison reform, and public sanitation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the Enlightenment's ideas, the reformers looked to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">
        Scientific Revolution
       </a>
       and industrial progress to solve the social problems which arose with the Industrial Revolution. Newton's natural philosophy combined a mathematics of axiomatic proof with the mechanics of physical observation, yielding a coherent system of verifiable predictions and replacing a previous reliance on revelation and inspired truth. Applied to public life, this approach yielded several successful campaigns for changes in social policy.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_Russia">
        Imperial Russia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Imperial Russia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">
        Russian Empire
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Under
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" title="Peter I of Russia">
        Peter I
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        the Great
       </i>
       ), Russia was proclaimed an Empire in 1721 and became recognized as a world power. Ruling from 1682 to 1725, Peter defeated Sweden in the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">
        Great Northern War
       </a>
       , forcing it to cede West
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">
        Karelia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ingria" title="Ingria">
        Ingria
       </a>
       (two regions lost by Russia in the
       <a href="/wiki/Time_of_Troubles" title="Time of Troubles">
        Time of Troubles
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Estonia" title="Governorate of Estonia">
        Estland
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livland" title="Livland">
        Livland
       </a>
       , securing Russia's access to the sea and sea trade.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">
        Baltic Sea
       </a>
       Peter founded a new capital called
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">
        Saint Petersburg
       </a>
       , later known as Russia's
       <i>
        Window to Europe
       </i>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great%27s_reforms" title="Peter the Great's reforms">
        Peter the Great's reforms
       </a>
       brought considerable Western European cultural influences to Russia.
       <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">
        Catherine II
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        the Great
       </i>
       ), who ruled in 1762–96, extended Russian political control over the Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth and incorporated most of its territories into Russia during the
       <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">
        Partitions of Poland
       </a>
       , pushing the Russian frontier westward into Central Europe. In the south, after successful
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_Wars" title="Russo-Turkish Wars">
        Russo-Turkish Wars
       </a>
       against the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       , Catherine advanced Russia's boundary to the Black Sea, defeating the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_khanate" title="Crimean khanate">
        Crimean khanate
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="European_dominance_and_the_19th_century">
        European dominance and the 19th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: European dominance and the 19th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">
        19th century
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">
        International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg">
         <img alt="Painting of a group of standing and seated heads of state in a variety of national uniforms and formal dress" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1274" data-file-width="2434" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg/400px-World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg/600px-World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg/800px-World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_heads_of_state_in_1889.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         "The World's Sovereigns", 1889.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Historians define the 19th century
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_time_periods" title="List of time periods">
        historical era
       </a>
       as stretching from 1815 (the
       <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">
        Congress of Vienna
       </a>
       ) to 1914 (the outbreak of the
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        First World War
       </a>
       ); alternatively,
       <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm" title="Eric Hobsbawm">
        Eric Hobsbawm
       </a>
       defined the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_long_19th_century" title="The long 19th century">
        "Long Nineteenth Century"
       </a>
       as spanning the years 1789 to 1914.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Imperialism_and_empires">
        Imperialism and empires
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Imperialism and empires">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">
        Imperialism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 1800s and early 1900s, once great and powerful Empires such as Spain, Ottoman Turkey, the Mughal Empire, and the Kingdom of Portugal began to break apart. Spain, which was at one time unrivaled in Europe, had been declining for a long time when it was crippled by Napoleon Bonaparte's invasion. Sensing the time was right, Spain's vast colonies in South America began a series of rebellions that ended with almost all of the Spanish territories gaining their independence.
      </p>
      <p>
       The once mighty Ottoman Empire was wracked with a series of revolutions, resulting with the Ottoman's only holding a small region that surrounded the capital, Istanbul.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Mughal empire, which was descended from the Mongol Khanate, was bested by the upcoming
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maratha_Confederacy" title="Maratha Confederacy">
        Maratha Confederacy
       </a>
       . All was going well for the
       <a href="/wiki/Maratha" title="Maratha">
        Marathas
       </a>
       until the British took an interest in the riches of India and the British ended up ruling not just the boundaries of Modern India, but also Pakistan, Burma, Nepal, Bangladesh and some Southern Regions of Afghanistan.
      </p>
      <p>
       The King of Portugal's vast territory of Brazil reformed into the independent Empire of Brazil.
      </p>
      <p>
       With the defeat of Napoleonic France, Britain became undoubtedly the most powerful country in the world, and by the end of the First World War controlled a Quarter of the world's population and a third of its surface. However, the power of the British Empire did not end on land, since it had the greatest navy on the planet.
      </p>
      <p>
       Electricity, steel, and petroleum enabled Germany to become a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(international)" title="Power (international)">
        great international power
       </a>
       that
       <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">
        raced to create empires of its own
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">
        Meiji Restoration
       </a>
       was a chain of events that led to enormous changes in Japan's political and social structure that was taking a firm hold at the beginning of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_Era" title="Meiji Era">
        Meiji Era
       </a>
       which coincided the opening of Japan by the arrival of the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Ships" title="Black Ships">
        Black Ships
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodore_(USN)" title="Commodore (USN)">
        Commodore
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">
        Matthew Perry
       </a>
       and made
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Japan" title="Imperial Japan">
        Imperial Japan
       </a>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">
        great power
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">
        Qing Dynasty
       </a>
       China failed to keep pace with the other world powers which led to massive social unrest in both empires. The Qing Dynasty's military power weakened during the 19th century, and faced with international pressure, massive
       <a href="/wiki/Rebellion" title="Rebellion">
        rebellions
       </a>
       and defeats in wars, the dynasty declined after the mid-19th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       European powers controlled parts of Oceania, with French
       <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">
        New Caledonia
       </a>
       from 1853 and
       <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">
        French Polynesia
       </a>
       from 1889; the Germans established colonies in
       <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">
        New Guinea
       </a>
       in 1884, and
       <a href="/wiki/German_Samoa" title="German Samoa">
        Samoa
       </a>
       in 1900.
      </p>
      <p>
       The United States expanded into the Pacific with Hawaii becoming a
       <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_Hawaii" title="Territory of Hawaii">
        U.S. territory
       </a>
       from 1898.
      </p>
      <p>
       Disagreements between the US, Germany and UK over Samoa led to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripartite_Convention_(1899)" title="Tripartite Convention (1899)">
        Tripartite Convention of 1899
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronology_of_colonialism" title="Chronology of colonialism">
        Chronology of colonialism
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="British_Victorian_era">
        British Victorian era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: British Victorian era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
        British Empire
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">
        Victorian era
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         National flag of the United Kingdom.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Victorian era of the United Kingdom was the period of
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
        Queen Victoria
       </a>
       's reign from June 1837 to January 1901. This was a long period of prosperity for the British people, as profits gained from the overseas British Empire, as well as from industrial improvements at home, allowed a large, educated middle class to develop. Some scholars would extend the beginning of the period—as defined by a variety of sensibilities and political games that have come to be associated with the Victorians—back five years to the passage of the
       <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">
        Reform Act 1832
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1897.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="849" data-file-width="1116" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/British_Empire_1897.jpg/220px-British_Empire_1897.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/British_Empire_1897.jpg/330px-British_Empire_1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/British_Empire_1897.jpg/440px-British_Empire_1897.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1897.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The British Empire in 1897, marked in the traditional colour for imperial British dominions on maps
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In Britain's "imperial century",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       victory over Napoleon left Britain without any serious international rival, other than Russia in central Asia. Unchallenged at sea, Britain adopted the role of global policeman, a state of affairs later known as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">
         Pax Britannica
        </a>
       </i>
       , and a foreign policy of "
       <a href="/wiki/Splendid_isolation" title="Splendid isolation">
        splendid isolation
       </a>
       ". Alongside the formal control it exerted over its own colonies, Britain's dominant position in world trade meant that it effectively controlled the economies of many nominally independent countries, such as China,
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
        Siam
       </a>
       , which has been generally characterized as "
       <a href="/wiki/Informal_empire" title="Informal empire">
        informal empire
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of note during this time was the
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">
        Anglo-Zulu War
       </a>
       , which was fought in 1879 between the British Empire and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zulu_Empire" title="Zulu Empire">
        Zulu Empire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       British imperial strength was underpinned by the
       <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">
        steamship
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Telegraphy" title="Telegraphy">
        telegraph
       </a>
       , new technologies invented in the second half of the 19th century, allowing it to control and defend the Empire. By 1902, the British Empire was linked together by a network of telegraph cables, the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/All_Red_Line" title="All Red Line">
        All Red Line
       </a>
       . Growing until 1922, around 13,000,000 square miles (34,000,000 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ) of territory and roughly 458 million people were added to the British Empire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The British established colonies in Australia in 1788, New Zealand in 1840 and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonial_Fiji" title="Colonial Fiji">
        Fiji
       </a>
       in 1872, with much of
       <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">
        Oceania
       </a>
       becoming part of the British Empire.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="French_governments_and_conflicts">
        French governments and conflicts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: French governments and conflicts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Restoration" title="Bourbon Restoration">
        Bourbon Restoration
       </a>
       followed the ousting of Napoleon I of France in 1814. The Allies restored the Bourbon Dynasty to the French throne. The ensuing period is called the Restoration, following French usage, and is characterized by a sharp conservative reaction and the re-establishment of the Roman Catholic Church as a power in French politics. The
       <a href="/wiki/July_Monarchy" title="July Monarchy">
        July Monarchy
       </a>
       was a period of liberal constitutional monarchy in France under King Louis-Philippe starting with the July Revolution (or Three Glorious Days) of 1830 and ending with the Revolution of 1848. The
       <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">
        Second Empire
       </a>
       was the Imperial Bonapartist regime of Napoleon III from 1852 to 1870, between the Second Republic and the Third Republic, in France.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="905" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg/220px-BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg/330px-BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg/440px-BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BismarckundNapoleonIII.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_III_of_France" title="Napoleon III of France">
          Napoleon III
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">
          Bismarck
         </a>
         after the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sedan" title="Battle of Sedan">
          Battle of Sedan
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">
        Franco-Prussian War
       </a>
       was a conflict between France and Prussia, while Prussia was backed up by the North German Confederation, of which it was a member, and the South German states of Baden, Württemberg and Bavaria. The complete Prussian and German victory brought about the final unification of Germany under King Wilhelm I of Prussia. It also marked the downfall of Napoleon III and the end of the Second French Empire, which was replaced by the Third Republic. As part of the settlement, almost all of the territory of Alsace-Lorraine was taken by Prussia to become a part of Germany, which it would retain until the end of World War I.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">
        French Third Republic
       </a>
       was the republican government of France between the end of the Second French Empire following the defeat of Louis-Napoléon in the Franco-Prussian war in 1870 and the Vichy Regime after the invasion of France by the German Third Reich in 1940. The Third Republic endured seventy years, making it the most long-lasting regime in France since the collapse of the Ancien Régime in the French Revolution of 1789.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_and_abolition">
        Slavery and abolition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Slavery and abolition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">
        Abolitionism
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
        Slavery
       </a>
       was greatly reduced around the world in the 19th century. Following a successful
       <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">
        slave revolt in Haiti
       </a>
       , Britain forced the
       <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">
        Barbary pirates
       </a>
       to halt their practice of kidnapping and enslaving Europeans,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act" title="Slavery Abolition Act">
        banned slavery throughout its domain
       </a>
       , and charged its navy with ending the global
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">
        slave trade
       </a>
       . Slavery was then abolished in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emancipation_reform_of_1861_in_Russia" title="Emancipation reform of 1861 in Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">
        America
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lei_%C3%81urea" title="Lei Áurea">
        Brazil
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="African_colonization">
        African colonization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: African colonization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Following the abolition of the slave trade in 1807 and propelled by economic exploitation, the
       <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">
        Scramble for Africa
       </a>
       was initiated formally at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_West_Africa_Conference" title="Berlin West Africa Conference">
        Berlin West Africa Conference
       </a>
       in 1884–1885. The Berlin Conference attempted to avoid war among the European powers by allowing the European rival countries to carve up the continent of Africa into national colonies. Africans were not consulted.
      </p>
      <p>
       The major European powers laid claim to the areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       where they could exhibit a sphere of influence over the area. These claims did not have to have any substantial land holdings or treaties to be legitimate. The European power that demonstrated its control over a territory accepted the mandate to rule that region as a national
       <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">
        colony
       </a>
       . The European nation that held the claim developed and benefited from their colony’s commercial interests without having to fear rival European competition. With the colonial claim came the underlying assumption that the European power that exerted control would use its mandate to offer protection and provide welfare for its colonial peoples, however, this principle remained more theory than practice. There were many documented instances of material and moral conditions deteriorating for native Africans in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries under European colonial rule, to the point where the colonial experience for them has been described as "hell on earth."
      </p>
      <p>
       At the time of the
       <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">
        Berlin Conference
       </a>
       , Africa contained one-fifth of the world’s population living in one-quarter of the world’s land area. However, from Europe's perspective, they were dividing an unknown continent. European countries established a few coastal colonies in Africa by the mid-nineteenth century, which included
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">
        Cape Colony
       </a>
       (Great Britain),
       <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
        Angola
       </a>
       (Portugal), and
       <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">
        Algeria
       </a>
       (France), but until the late nineteenth century Europe largely traded with free African states without feeling the need for territorial possession. Until the 1880s most of Africa remained uncharted, with western maps from the period generally showing blank spaces for the continent’s interior.
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 1880s to 1914, the European powers expanded their control across the African continent, competing with each other for Africa’s land and resources.
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">
        Great Britain
       </a>
       controlled various colonial holdings in East Africa that spanned the length of the African continent from Egypt in the north to South Africa. The
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        French
       </a>
       gained major ground in West Africa, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       held colonies in southern Africa.
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       established a small number of colonies at various points throughout the continent, which included German East Africa (Tanganyika) and German Southwest Africa for Germany, Eritrea and Libya for Italy, and the Canary Islands and Rio de Oro in northwestern Africa for Spain. Finally, for
       <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="Leopold II of Belgium">
        King Leopold
       </a>
       (ruled from 1865 to 1909), there was the large “piece of that great African cake” known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">
        Congo
       </a>
       , which, unfortunately
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Opinion" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:ASSERT" title="Wikipedia:ASSERT">
          <span title="This statement may be opinion presented as fact (July 2017)">
           opinion
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       for the native Congolese, became his personal fiefdom to do with as he pleased in Central Africa. By 1914, almost the entire continent was under European control.
       <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">
        Liberia
       </a>
       , which was settled by freed American slaves in the 1820s, and Abyssinia (
       <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">
        Ethiopia
       </a>
       ) in eastern Africa were the last remaining independent African states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Meiji_Japan">
        Meiji Japan
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Meiji Japan">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-style ambox-confusing" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/20px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/30px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           may be
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness">
            confusing or unclear
           </a>
           to readers
          </b>
          . In particular, the 1960s are mentioned in a paragraph about an era that ended in the early 1900s..
          <small>
           <i>
            (July 2016)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Around the end of the 19th century and into the 20th century, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_era" title="Meiji era">
        Meiji era
       </a>
       was marked by the reign of the
       <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">
        Meiji Emperor
       </a>
       . During this time, Japan started its modernization and rose to world power status. This
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_era_name" title="Japanese era name">
        era name
       </a>
       means "Enlightened Rule". In Japan, the Meiji Restoration started in the 1860s, marking the rapid modernization by the Japanese themselves along European lines. Much research has focused on the issues of discontinuity versus continuity with the previous Tokugawa Period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1960s younger Japanese scholars led by Irokawa Daikichi, reacted against the bureaucratic superstate, and began searching for the historic role of the common people . They avoided the elite, and focused not on political events but on social forces and attitudes. They rejected both Marxism and modernization theory as alien and confining. They stressed the importance of popular energies in the development of modern Japan. They enlarged history by using the methods of
       <a href="/wiki/Social_history" title="Social history">
        social history
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was not until the beginning of the Meiji Era that the Japanese government began taking modernization seriously. Japan expanded its military production base by opening arsenals in various locations. The hyobusho (war office) was replaced with a War Department and a Naval Department. The
       <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">
        samurai
       </a>
       class suffered great disappointment the following years.
      </p>
      <p>
       Laws were instituted that required every able-bodied male Japanese citizen, regardless of class, to serve a mandatory term of three years with the first reserves and two additional years with the second reserves. This action, the deathblow for the samurai warriors and their
       <i>
        daimyōs
       </i>
       , initially met resistance from both the peasant and warrior alike. The peasant class interpreted the term for military service, ketsu-eki ("blood tax") literally, and attempted to avoid service by any means necessary. The Japanese government began modelling their ground forces after the French military. The French government contributed greatly to the training of Japanese officers. Many were employed at the military academy in Kyoto, and many more still were feverishly translating French field manuals for use in the Japanese ranks.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the death of the Meiji Emperor, the
       <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taish%C5%8D" title="Emperor Taishō">
        Taishō Emperor
       </a>
       took the throne, thus beginning the
       <a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" title="Taishō period">
        Taishō period
       </a>
       . A key foreign observer of the remarkable and rapid changes in
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">
        Japanese society
       </a>
       in this period was
       <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Mason_Satow" title="Ernest Mason Satow">
        Ernest Mason Satow
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Representative Western scholars include George Akita,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/William_G._Beasley" title="William G. Beasley">
        William Beasley
       </a>
       , James B. Crowley,
       <a href="/wiki/John_W._Dower" title="John W. Dower">
        John W. Dower
       </a>
       , Peter Duus,
       <a href="/wiki/Carol_Gluck" title="Carol Gluck">
        Carol Gluck
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_Herbert" title="Norman Herbert">
        Norman Herbert
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/John_W._Hall" title="John W. Hall">
        John W. Hall
       </a>
       , Mikiso Hane,
       <a href="/wiki/Akira_Iriye" title="Akira Iriye">
        Akira Iriye
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Marius_Jansen" title="Marius Jansen">
        Marius Jansen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Edwin_O._Reischauer" title="Edwin O. Reischauer">
        Edwin O. Reischauer
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_B._Sansom" title="George B. Sansom">
        George B. Sansom
       </a>
       , Bernard Silberman, Richard Storry, Karel van Wolfram, and
       <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Vogel" title="Ezra Vogel">
        Ezra Vogel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
        United States
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: United States">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_(1865%E2%80%931918)" title="History of the United States (1865–1918)">
        History of the United States (1865–1918)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Territorial_evolution_of_North_America_since_1763" title="Territorial evolution of North America since 1763">
        Territorial evolution of North America since 1763
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">
        Colonial history of the United States
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Wars" title="American Indian Wars">
        American Indian Wars
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States#19th_century" title="Native Americans in the United States">
        Native Americans in the United States § 19th century
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=19th-century_North_American_Natives&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="19th-century North American Natives (page does not exist)">
        19th-century North American Natives
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Antebellum_expansion">
        Antebellum expansion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Antebellum expansion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="528" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg/220px-Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg/330px-Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg/440px-Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westward_the_Course_of_Empire.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         American westward expansion is idealized in
         <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Leutze" title="Emanuel Leutze">
          Emanuel Leutze
         </a>
         's famous painting
         <i>
          Westward the Course of Empire Takes its Way
         </i>
         (1861).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/American_frontier" title="American frontier">
        American frontier
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_changes_of_the_United_States" title="Territorial changes of the United States">
        Territorial changes of the United States
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antebellum_Age" title="Antebellum Age">
        Antebellum Age
       </a>
       was a period of increasing division in the country based on the growth of slavery in the
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
        American South
       </a>
       and in the western territories of
       <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">
        Kansas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">
        Nebraska
       </a>
       that eventually lead to the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
        Civil War
       </a>
       in 1861. The Antebellum Period is often considered to have begun with the
       <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">
        Kansas–Nebraska Act
       </a>
       of 1854,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       although it may have begun as early as 1812. This period is also significant because it marked the transition of American manufacturing to the industrial revolution.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">
        Manifest destiny
       </a>
       " was the belief that the United States was destined to expand across the North American continent, from the Atlantic seaboard to the Pacific Ocean. During this time, the United States expanded to the Pacific Ocean—"from sea to shining sea"—largely defining the borders of the contiguous United States as they are today.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War_and_Reconstruction">
        Civil War and Reconstruction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_history&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Civil War and Reconstruction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
        American Civil War
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">
        Reconstruction era of the United States
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The American Civil War came when seven (later eleven)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_state" title="Slave state">
        Southern slave states
       </a>
       declared their
       <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">
        secession
       </a>
       from the U.S. and formed the
       <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">
        Confederate States of America
       </a>
